%This article is editted by \LaTeX \\ %
\title{Four Essays on Labor and Development Economics \\ {\normalsize A dissertation submitted in partial fulfillment of the requirements for the degree of Doctor of Philosophy
(Economics) }
}
\author{Yiqian Lu \\ State University of New York at Buffalo}
\date{Fall 2018}

\maketitle
\clearpage
\begin{abstract}
    This dissertation consists of four chapters on the topics of labor and development economics. Chapter one discusses the innovation fluctuation in an aging economy. A three-stage overlapping generations model is constructed to mimic the population trend and its consequent impact on innovation and economics development. Both the theoretical analysis and empirical modification shows that countries with low fertility rate have higher innovation rate in the short period but lower rate in the long time horizon. This chapter discusses the negative impact of an aging economy and is lean to strong policy implication of increasing subsidy to increase the fertility rate, particularly for those developed European countries suffering from aging problem in the foreseeable future. Chapter two focuses on the relationship between unemployment rate, working efficiency and work-over-time problem. It is motivated by the widely observed phenomenon in some east Asian countries and consulting, investment banking sectors in the United States. Does working overtime indeed produce extra efficiency? I use the Panel Study of Income Dynamics (PSID) data to answer this question. The answer is NO and it has little impact on the unemployment rate. This chapter contributes to existing literature with a new empirical approach. It could also guide corporate management professionals to enact proper overtime working policy. Chapter three debates the heated-discussion of the relationship between institutions and economics outcome. The prestigious MIT and Chicago political economist Daron Acemoglu and James Robinson argues that only inclusive political and economics institution could lead to economic prosperity. Other scholars dispute their theory don’t catch the whole picture. Bill Gates said the theory of Acemoglu and Robinson must take into account other important factors and this chapter contributes to this gap. I find that economics prosperity could be harvested even in the extractive institutions with immigration-friendly policies. That said, countries like United Arab Emirates, Qatar and Singapore could enjoy the long-term economics boom with good immigration policy by attracting high-skilled and low-skilled immigrants of their Neighbors. Chapter four intends to figure out the empirical relationship between income inequality and consumption inequality in China. Existing empirical literature show the consumption inequality outnumbers income inequality in China, which contradicts basic economics theory as well as evidence from US, Canada and Europe. I use the inverse relationship between necessity good consumption and income to impute the true income level of each individual. High income communist party members and government-related agency employees tend to hide their grey income and the income inequality is thus underestimated. The real income-consumption inequality relationship is consistent with the empirical evident in other countries.

\end{abstract}
\clearpage
\textbf{JEL:} E23, J13, J22, J24, J31, L26, O11, P16, P23, P26 

\textbf{Keywords:} low fertility, human capital, working overtime, ability signal, inclusive institutions, income inequality, consumption inequality

\begin{section*}{\textbf{Preface}}


It’s a long journey to pursuit my Doctorate Degree in Economics. I would like to express my sincere gratefulness to my advisor Dr. Neel Rao, for his continuous support on my dissertation as well as the pursuit of truth and happiness of Economics Science. My other current and past committee members Dr. Mingliang Li, Dr. Yun Pei and Dr. Goncalo Monteiro also educated me several important classes and encouraged me during difficulties. 

Economics is really a beautiful subject fulfilled with rigorous logical thinking, elegant quantitative modelling and detailed empirical verification. My dissertation is composed of four chapters, focusing on the a variety of topics of labor economics and applied econometric. 

Chapter one discusses the innovation fluctuation in an aging economy. A three-stage overlapping generations model is constructed to mimic the population trend and its consequent impact on innovation and economics development. Both the theoretical analysis and empirical modification shows that countries with low fertility rate have higher innovation rate in the short period but lower rate in the long time horizon. This chapter discusses the negative impact of an aging economy and is lean to strong policy implication of increasing subsidy to increase the fertility rate, particularly for those developed European countries suffering from aging problem in the foreseeable future.

Chapter two focuses on the relationship between unemployment rate, working efficiency and work-over-time problem. It is motivated by the widely observed phenomenon in some east Asian countries and consulting, investment banking sectors in the United States. Does working overtime indeed produce extra efficiency? I use the Panel Study of Income Dynamics (PSID) data to answer this question. The answer is NO and it has little impact on the unemployment rate. This chapter contributes to existing literature with a new empirical approach. It could also guide corporate management professionals to enact proper overtime working policy.

Chapter three debates the heated-discussion of the relationship between institutions and economics outcome. The prestigious MIT and Chicago political economist Daron Acemoglu and James Robinson argues that only inclusive political and economics institution could lead to economic prosperity. Other scholars dispute their theory don’t catch the whole picture. Bill Gates said the theory of Acemoglu and Robinson must take into account other important factors and this chapter contributes to this gap. I find that economics prosperity could be harvested even in the extractive institutions with immigration-friendly policies. That said, countries like United Arab Emirates, Qatar and Singapore could enjoy the long-term economics boom with good immigration policy by attracting high-skilled and low-skilled immigrants of their Neighbors. 

Chapter four intends to figure out the empirical relationship between income inequality and consumption inequality in China. Existing empirical literature show the consumption inequality outnumbers income inequality in China, which contradicts basic economics theory as well as evidence from US, Canada and Europe. I use the inverse relationship between necessity good consumption and income to impute the true income level of each individuals. High income communist party members and government-related agency employees tend to hide their grey income and the income inequality is thus underestimated. The real income-consumption inequality relationship is consistent with the empirical evident in other countries.

I wish to thank the department of Economics of University at Buffalo providing financial support during my graduate study. This dissertation benefits from many heated discussion with my fellow classmates Xu Guo, Yiru Wang, Yenan Sun, Bibaswan Chatterjee and especially Jinquan Gong. I presented some chapters of my dissertation at MassPRIM investment board, Analysis Group, Midwest Economics Conference and all the remarks from Eric Nierenberg, Bill Li, Edward Wang, Michael Trotsky, Mike McGirr, Michael Bailey, Na Datson, Ching Watson, Xuan Jiang and Xiaosheng Mu. My friends Jun Chu, Rujie Yin, Zhimo Shen, Menghui Wang, Heming Shou, Yuanpei Cao provides first-hand knowledge, continuous encouragement and detailed feedback from various angles to my dissertation. I also treasure the legacy of Professor Gary Becker from the University of Chicago. Becker's work has traversed the entire gamut of the social sciences and demonstrates the elegance and simplicity of economics to me.

Last but not the least, I would like to thank my parents Guoqiang Lu, Dongmei Qian and my wife Jincheng Zhang, who give me live and nurture me from my childhood and college. My dissertation could not be finished without their love. 


\end{section}
\clearpage

\begin{section} {Chapter 1: Population Structure and Innovation Level}
\subsection{Introduction}


Income, inequality and innovation level remain in the central topics of the economics development. From Adam Smith (The Wealth of Nations, 1776) to Acemoglu (Why Nations Fail, 2012), thousands of scholars tend to discuss this pivotal topic from culture background, geographic influence, climate change, political roles, education influence, etc.. In the more recent studies, people tend to illustrate this crucial topic from the aspect of population structure and fertility rate (James Liang 2010, Wenzheng Huang, Jianxing Li 2012, Fuxian Yi 2013). Current literature (Isaac Ehrlich and Jinyoung Kim, 2004) pointed out an inverted-U sharped relationship between the output per capita and fertility rate, which could be empirically demonstrated by the survey data around the world. It could be explained that the fertility rate grows gradually because of the better medical condition and nutrition when the output per capita starts at a very low level while drops when output per capital exceeds a threshold because of the high opportunity cost (promotion opportunity, leisure, pecuniary expenditure loss) of raising kids. 

The majority literature of economics development(Solow 1957, Romer 1986, etc.) discuss the issues on growth rate, economic inequality and social welfare under the steady population growth rate or constant population structure. This is mainly because the world experiences  steady population growth after WWII. Baby boomers after the end of the Second World War contribute to the economy throughout most developed and developing countries. However, the picture of the next few decades don't guarantee us the assumption that we have a steady population growth rate and a nice overlapping generation structure anymore. Most developed countries experienced low birth rate in the recent 20 years such as Japan(1.40), South Korea (1.25) and Singapore (0.81) according to CIA data. And even worse, some developing countries experienced dramatic fertility drop under relentless birth control policies. For instance, the fertility rate of China drops from 5.5 in 1960s to 1.18  in the year 2010 (2010 Population Census, National Bureau of Statistics of China) under the One-Child Policy. Uzbekistan government removed uterus of women who gave birth to 2 children under the dictatorship of Islam Karimov (Acemoglu 2012).  These short-sighted anti-human policies crush out the light of future economics growth in those developing countries. (Acemoglu 2012, Joe Biden and Hilliary Clinton 2013, James Liang 2010, ).

In this paper, I attempt to figure out how a rational person make selection between the rational choice of  raising more kids, starting up a new business and working as a employee for paycheck during his adulthood life. I believe the  low fertility  phenomenon that is observed in most developed countries and some developing countries are because of the following reasons. \\
(1) The compulsory education system and the increasing popularity of college education lower the length of possible childbearing years. Typically speaking a female has 15 to 20 years of possible childbearing age while modern education system (college level and graduate level education) reduced this period by 3 to 5 years.\\
(2) The opportunity cost of giving birth to next generation is high with the wage compensation increasing. In other word, the shadow price paying to bear a child increases with economics development. The opportunity cost rockets under the background that the whole society suffers a low fertility rate. It is estimated (Gary Becker 1986, James Liang 2012) that the marginal cost of raising an additional kid in a high fertility country is far less than in a low fertility one. Parents would race on the education competition if they have only one child. The win-or-lose-all altitude leads to a significant price hike on child-bearing and education. As a result, if a country stepped into low fertility dilemma, it is extremely hard for this country to get rid of it. For instance, those countries where experience low fertility encourage bearing more children by providing thousands of dollars subsidies (Korea, Japan, Singapore) but the policy does not make sense as thousands of dollars subsidies are way too less. \\
(3) People's preference are more realistic and less altruistic to the next generation with economics development. That is to say, people are becoming careless about their children welfare after their own death since they cannot see the welfare anymore after their decease. More couples tend to become DINK (double income with no kids) since they care less about  next generation, particularly under a more developed pension and social security system.  Those countries having a worse social security system do not suffer from the low fertility since the incentive for raising more kids are enormous. From the famous soft budget constraint explanation (Yingyi Qian, 1996) approach, it could be explained people tend to misuse the social security (public resources from next generation) without paying enough expenditure (raising more kids), particularly in Europe.  People believe government will pay them after retirement even if they don't pay anything for next generation's tax revenue (the expenditure of raising kids).  The completeness and greatness of social security systems encourage "lazy" couples to raise less children, which paying less cost for future government tax revenue.

It is  widely believed (Gary Becker 1971, EA Hanushek 1992, H Li, J Zhang, Y Zhu 2008) that there  are trade off between the quantity and quality of children. Those scholars  argue with less kids there will be better education and human capital accumulation for offspring. As a result, the rational choice of the number of children should be on the balance point of marginal benefit of an additional kids equaling to marginal benefit of increasing quality of preexisting kids. However, more recent study (J Angrist, V Lavy, A Schlosser, 2010) points out there are no causal link between the quality and the quantity of the children. Wenzheng Huang and James Liang (2012) describe there are no statistical significant quality difference between the single child and non-single child, which is somehow counter-intuitive. The Nobel Laureate Gary Becker told his last student James Liang before his passing that even if there is trade-off, it is still extremely wrong and silly for Chinese government to adopt the One Child Policy. Feng Wang, Y Cai, B Gu (2013) argued that history will give more negative judgment on Chinese absurd One Child Policy in the next few decades, which "is even worse than Culture Revolution and  Planned Economy system". The increase on quality of children could never be compensated by the loss of huge population potentials.


Gary Becker (1994) pointed out society could save across generations by the birth of many children. Particularly, the between-generation return to  human capital investment is much higher than physical capital if the education system is well-functioned. The economic growth is majorly driven by the between generation human capital accumulation. As a result, more children under a well-functioned education system could contribute greatly to the long-term economic growth. By comparison, the short-term economics growth is majorly driven by the within-generation TFP increase. (JY Lin, R Ren 2007, J Huang, JY Lin, S Rozelle 1999 ,L Yuhong, W Hao, Z Yuxin 2008)


James Liang (2012) conducted very important research between the entrepreneurship and population structure. As a successful Nasdaq-listed high-tech company founder, a billionaire and an active top-economics-journal publisher,  he concludes that the social ladder for younger will vanish gradually under a low fertility rate and inversed-triangle population pyramid.  This is particularly observed in Japan (K Christensen, G Doblhammer, R Rau 2009, N Ogawa, A Mason, A Chawla, R Matsukura 2009, LJ Schoppa, 2001) under Japanese reluctant policy-reforming system. On the contrary, the younger generation of India and United States could quickly accumulate adequate human capital to pave the way for business success thanks to the percentage of old cohort being very small (the population pyramid of USA and India are triangle-shaped). Sillicon Valley, for instance, has witnessed (AL Saxenian 2005) the booming of Indian-originated entrepreneurs. The head leader of Google, Microsoft, Adobe, Cisco are all Indian originated and meanwhile first generation Indian Americans have occupied almost half of the Vice President level positions in FLAG (Facebook, Linkedin, Amazon, Google).




The organization of this paper is as follows. In section 2 we tend to build a three-period overlapping generation model to depict the dynamic optimal path between the choice of raising more kids, involving in a start-up business or working for paycheck. The transitional process will be discussed specifically under different preference assumptions.  The influence on the economy under low fertility condition will be demonstrated.  In section 3 we describe the benchmark empirical model and the data set. The statistical relationship between the low fertility and the economics growth rate, inequality and innovation level will be displayed. Also, we focus on inflation and unemployment issue to depict the impact of low fertility rate more specifically. In section 4 we conduct three types of robustness check and discuss the empirical result in detailed pattern. Section 5 concludes the whole paper and gives future policy suggestions.

\subsection{Theoretical Approach}

\subsubsection{OLG Model Setup}

Following Isaac Ehrlich and Jinyoung Kim (2007) pattern, we consider a three period overlapping generation mode in a economy with a representative agent. The represented agent's total utility depends on his consumption. In this article we suppose the agent lives through childhood, adult and the elder age. All the production are produced by people who are  adulthood currently. We assume no government involvement in our model. As a result, we can never use fiscal policy to collect tax revenue from each family and redistribute to the society in a certain form.

In the childhood, young kids live completely on their parents. In the model,  denote $y_t$ as the to total output of each parent. Each parent spends $\theta_t/2$ of their individual output to raise their kids. (So altogether mom and dad spend $\theta_t$ of their individual output.) As a result one kid can consume $y_t \theta_t$ during his childhood life. $C_{1,t}$ is used to represent childhood consumption when the agent is a child in period t. 


Then the represent agent grows up from childhood to adulthood. We suppose a survival rate $\pi_1$, which means the representative agent has a chance of $1-\pi_1$ that he cannot survive the childhood age. Here we make the general assumption that $0<\pi_1<1$. The representative agent chooses whether to make a new business (e.g. a start-up company) or work normally as an employee for paycheck compensation. 
 If the producer decides to make a start-up he/she will lose the opportunity chance to receive biweekly paycheck, a source of steady income. We assume the new business level (entrepreneurship) has a non-negative effect on the production efficiency (paycheck wage ratio). With different social aggregate production level, the innovation level and entrepreneurship exhibit increasing marginal production efficiency effect under larger aggregate output level.  Also during adulthood life the agent needs to decide how many offspring to raise. The reason of having children is quite natural since human being, as a species of mammal, wishes to make the gene live forever by raising kids. In our model, we assume during pregnancy and lactation period, the represented agent cannot work or start a new business to increase their utility level. During this period, parents have to devote themselves thoroughly in  the
 birth to a new life. In the model I make a reasonable assumption that an adult can only breed children during adulthood time. 

During the adulthood life, besides an investment in raising kid, the representative agent also need to pay their parents pension expenses as there is no government involvement in this model. That is to say, if parents of the agent are still alive, there should exist a portion deduction of the total output of this Representative agent to raise  parents since parents in their elder age are retired and do not make further production. 

There is a probably $\pi_2$ that an adulthood can survive to his/her old age. It is quite similar to $\pi_1$ case that we may assume $0<\pi_2<1$ and make it exogenous. When a person becomes older he/she stops working and lives only on his/her pension from  their children. In this particular model the pension is collected from the next generation of the family. Parents raise kids when they are young and lives on their grown-up children when older and becomes no longer suitable for work.



A young parent chooses to maximize his/her lifetime utility as follows:
\be 
U=[\f{1}{1-\sigma}][C_{1,t}^{1-\sigma}-1]+\beta \pi_1 [ \f{1}{1-\sigma}] [ C_{2,t+1}^{1-\sigma}-1]+\beta^2 \pi_1 \pi_2 [\f{1}{1-\sigma}] [C_{3,t+2}^{1-\sigma}-1] \label{E1}
\ee
subject to 
\be
C_{1,t}=\theta_t y_t  \label{E2}
\ee
\be
y_t=A_t l_t+A_b b_t  \label{E3}
\ee
\be
m n_{t+1}\leq L_1  \label {E4}
\ee
\be
b_{t+1}+m n_{t+1}+l_{t+1}\leq L_2 \label {E5}
\ee
\be 
\pi_1=\pi_1(Y_t)   \label {E6}
\ee
\be
\pi_2=\pi_2(Y_t)  \label {E7}
\ee
\be
B_t=\sum_{k=1}^{N_t} b_k  \label {E8}
\ee
\be
Y_t=N_t y_t   \label {E9}
\ee
\be
C_{2,t+1}=f_{t+1} w_{t+1} l_{t+1}  \label {E10}
\ee
\be
f_{t+1}=1- \f{1}{2} n_{t+1}  \theta_{t+1}-\pi_2 x_{t+1}    \label {E11}
\ee
\be
C_{3,t+2}=\f{1}{2}  \pi_1 x_{t+2}  n_{t+1} C_{2,t+2}   \label {E12}
\ee
\be    A_t=A B_t^\alpha=A (\sum_{k=1}^{N_t} b_k) ^\alpha    \label {E13} \ee
\be    A_b= p_t A B_t^\gamma=p_t A (\sum_{k=1}^{N_t} b_k) ^\gamma \label{E14} \ee



The choice variables here are $\{n_t\}_{t=0}^\infty$,$\{\theta_t\}_{t=0}^\infty$,$\{x_t\}_{t=0}^\infty$, $\{b_t\}_{t=0}^\infty$, $\{l_t \}_{t=0}^\infty$
\\

Since the wage rate are competitively determined we have
\be
w_t=\f{\partial}{\partial{l_t}} y_t= \f{\partial}{\partial{l_t}} [ A B_t^\alpha l_t]=A B_t^\alpha \label {E15}
\ee

In the expression (\ref{E1}), we assume a constant relative risk aversion utility function with respect to consumption in different periods. $\beta$ denotes the time preference. We makes no assumption on $\beta$. A representative agent might have $\beta>1$ just because he/she is more concerned with consumption in old age while in some other cases he/she may have $\beta<1$ since childhood life is the most important period in this case. % $\tilde{C}_{2,t+1} (e_{t+1})$ specifies the reputation gain.

In equation (\ref{E2}), $\theta_t$ is the portion of parent's income spending on each kid. In out notation, $C_{1,t}=\theta_t y_t$ is the total consumption in the agent's childhood life. The agent can only live on his/her parents' income when he/she is in the young age. 

In equation (\ref{E3}), $l_t$ is the total time the representative producer chooses to work for paycheck. $b_t$ denotes the total time the agent chooses to work for a new business (for example: a start-up high-tech company which is risky). $A_t$ is the marginal return on work while $A_b$ represents the marginal return on a start-up company. $A_t$ and $A_b$ are defined and their economic meaning are discussed in equation (\ref{E13}) and equation (\ref{E14}). Total outputs depends only on the return on regular work sector and innovative start-up sector. In this case, $b_t$ could be treated as venture capital which is definitely more risky. There are no cross-generation physical capital accumulation in this model.


In constraint (\ref{E4}), $L_1$ denotes the maximum year that a consumer can breed off-springs (usually approximately 15 years) and $n_t$ is the number of children(i.e. the fertility rate). Here $m$ is the parameter representing pregnancy and breed length of a single child. This parameter is obviously longer than 10 months but in more general cases it can be a period of one and a half to two years since a person must devote almost all his/her time and energy to the baby when the child is very young. It is well known that woman can hardly give birth to a child above a certain age. This is the most pivotal point in this inequality constraint and we will see it plays an important role in the fertility rate decision making process. Also, $m$ is the minimum years gap between two children.

In constraint (\ref{E5}), $L_2$ denotes the length of adulthood life.  The producer chooses during his adulthood how to separate his/her time into going for new business, raising kids and working for outputs. It is quite obvious the inequality constraint will become an equality constraints when doing optimization. The reason is that if the constraint is slack the agent can always increase the production time to increase his second period's utility level. Certainly we have $L_2>L_1$.

Through equations (\ref{E6}) and (\ref{E7}), two parameters $\pi_1$, $\pi_2$ are introduced, where $\pi_1$ is the survival rate from childhood to adult,$\pi_2$ defines the rate the agent has the luck to retire. These two parameter are supposed to be exogenous. In that case, it is assumed the surviving rates depend on the aggregate social output level . It shall be seriously taken into account  the importance of the rapid change of new business contribution to the whole society  that leads to the great improvement of production efficiency.

In equation (\ref{E8}) and (\ref{E9}). The social aggregate  new business level $B_t$ is the aggregate of individual new business input level $\sum b_t$.  $N_t$ defines total population of a generation and $Y_t$ represents social aggregate output level.

In equation (\ref{E10}) and (\ref{E11}), $C_{2,t+1}$ is the consumption of an adult in period $t+1$. In this three-period overlapping generation model we assume that the total consumption is a fraction of output. Each adult has to spend $\f{1}{2}\theta_t$ of total output to raise each single child.  In the model $x_t$ denotes the proportion that the consumer will give the output to parents if they are still alive. The expectation of such proportion is $\pi_2 x_t$. As no government is included in this model, it ensures the agent will have the basic necessities of life when having no ability to work anymore by receiving money from their children.

In equation (\ref{E12}), $C_{3,t+2}$ defines the consumption of an old person in period $t+2$. According to the previous explanation, the component of $C_{3,t+2}$ depends on the transferring payments from off-springs. The expected value of this part of consumption is the product of survival rate of the children, the number of kids, the output each child will transfer.

In equation (\ref{E13}), we make a crucial assumption that marginal return on work depends on the social aggregate venture capita level $B_t$. That is to say, we believe new business contribute so much and provide positive externality to the productivity rate. Here $\alpha$ and $A$ are constant.

In equation (\ref{E14}), we assume $p_t$ is the probability of running a start-up successfully and $1-p_t$ is the failure probability. Usually speaking, $p_t$ should be quite small (the succeed rate in US could be less than 10 \% while in China it could be even less than 5 \%) It should be expected that $p_t < 0.1$. We may assume
 \be p_t=p(Y_t) \f{N_t}{N_t+N_{t-1}} \ee 

where $p(Y_t)$ is a constant with $p'()=\epsilon>0$(very small but positive) and $ \f{N_t}{N_t+N_{t-1}}$ is percentage of the younger cohort it time $t$. We believe the success probability coefficient $p$ should increase with diminishing transaction cost under the economic development. Also, with more younger generation percentage, younger generation could have a higher chance to acquire a senior position to accumulate adequate human capital to pave the way for start-up success. (James Liang 2012) 
The total marginal return from a new business is defined as:
\be
A_b=\underbrace{p_t}_{Prob. \ of \  success} \underbrace{A B_t^\gamma}_{return\  from \ success}+\underbrace{(1-p_t)}_{Prob. \ of \  failure} \underbrace{0}_{return\  from \ failure}
\ee




Obviously we should have $\gamma>>\alpha$ where $0<\alpha<\gamma<1$, which is the incentive of successful business. Usually the return from seed stock and option bonus for a successful business should be more than  3000 \% of the salary compensation from the data of US stock market.

When we eliminate $\{b_t\}_{t=0}^\infty$ we have to solve the following problem:
\be
\begin{split}
\max_{\{n_t , \theta_t, x_t, l_t\}_{t=0}^\infty}  U &=[\f{1}{1-\sigma}] \{ [\theta_t(A_t l_t+A_b(L_2-l_t-m n_t)]^{1-\sigma}-1   \} \\ 
 &+ \beta \pi_1 \f{1}{1-\sigma} \{ [(A_t l_t +A_b (L_2-l_t- m n_t))(1-\f{1}{2}n_t \theta_{t+1} -\pi_2 x_{t+1})]^{1-\sigma} -1   \}  \\
&\beta^2 \pi_1 \pi_2 \{ [\f{1}{2} x_{t+2} \pi_1 n_{t+1} (A_t l_t +A_b(L_2-l_t-m n_t)) ]^{1-\sigma} -1 \}
\end{split}
\ee

For the purpose of simplicity and solubility,  we assume no savings and government participation in our framework. Relaxing the savings assumption, especially allowing parents to borrow money in the second period would, could potentially increase the budget constraints to spend on child-bearing and education. Under the case of limited financial borrowing capacity, government should increase the subsidy for young adults to help raise more kids with sufficient budgets, actively supplementing the incomplete credit market. Government intervention is particularly important for those non-immigrant countries, as they have to boost the fertility rate without external foreign immigrants.

 \end{subsection}

\subsubsection {The equilibrium path and economic explanation}

We assume that parents expect the proportion of children transfer payments $E(x_{t+2})$ is their own transfer payments proportion to the previous generation $x_{t+1}$. In this case, $x$ is not necessarily to be 0 at the equilibrium. 

Let the aggregate new business(venture) capital to output ratio in $t$ as $q_t$ and denote $\tilde{L_2}=L_2-m n_t$.\\ 
\textbf{Proposition 1.} With a fixed start-up succeed coefficient $p$ fixed, at the equilibrium we should have $q_t$ exist uniquely and $\f{\p q}{\p p}>0$. That is to say the increasing chance of new business success increases the proportion of new business investment.\\

Proofs of all propositions are provided at the end of this chapter

\textbf{Proposition 2.} With the increase of $\gamma$, $q$ will increase.  \\
\textbf{Proof.} It could be directly obtained from equation (\ref{*}). \qed\\
\textbf{Remark.} $p$ and $\gamma$ is the incentive for getting more revenue from successfully building a new business. So increasing the benefit or chance from success will increase the attempt for high return.  \\
\textbf{Proposition 3.} The new business/total output ratio $q$ will increase with current fertility $n_t$ decreases and last period fertility $n_{t-1}$ increases.\\

\textbf{Remark.} This proposition shows the dynamic effect of the fertility rate on innovation trial. Current child born will exert negative influence on current period innovation since the representative individual have to divide more time to take care of the children as equation (\ref{5}) states. However, the new born baby boomers will encourage the innovation trials (increase $q$) in the next generation because these babies become adults in the next period, which could enhance aggregate output level and consequently increases in the marginal benefit of a successful start-up company.
In this case, more people tend to enter the high-risky innovative sector to win a potential high payoff under a larger incentive. The dynamic effect of fertility rate is the central topic in the paper so that this proposition is really important for further derivation and discussion.\\
\textbf{Proposition 4.} If the preference for next generation $\beta$ is high enough, which indicates that the representative agent cares a lot for the future. Then the optimal fertility path will be as follows.
\be  n^*=\f{L_1}{m} \ee

\textbf{Remark.}  \\
\textbf{Proposition 4.} is just what we observed around the world in middle age or in some African countries nowadays. The aggregate economics development level is significantly low. Under the poor medical treatment and the consequently high death rate, human beings have to raise as many children as they can to proliferate. If mankind choose low feriltiy rate at that time, we human beings might have been distinct and could no longer have survived till today. But the child-bearing period has the maximum length of $L_1$ so that there exists a upper limit bound of the fertility rate. This proposition clarifies an individual keeps giving birth to new babies without interval when they are at child-bearing period before the industrial revolution when the time preference factor is significantly high. $\beta$, in a certain extent, defines human being's altruism for next generation. Before the industrial revolution, such altruism is so high that the population of human beings could keep growing, having a chance embrace the sunrise of technology rapid growth after-coming.  If human being could not survive until modern day when marginal revenue from new business is high enough to generate technology and TFP growth, it is meaningless for us to talk about the infinite-period utility maximization model.  \\

\textbf{Proposition 5.}  When \textbf{Proposition 4.} holds, the number of the live adults grows at a rate of $g^{max}=\f{\pi_1 L_1}{2m}-1$ for each generation. Denote $N_t$ as the living adult population in period $t$. It is clearly that $N_t=(1+g^{max}) N_{t-1}=(\f{\pi_1 L_1}{2m}-1)N_{t-1}$. $P_t$ is used to calculate the total population in period $t$. The population is calculated as follows:
\be
\begin{split}
 P_t &=\f{n_t}{2} N_t+ N_t+ \pi_2 N_{t-1}\\
        &=(\f{L_1}{2 m}+1 +\f{\pi_2}{\f{\pi_1 L_1}{2 m}-1})N_t
\end{split}
\ee
Also
\be
 P_{t+1} =(\f{L_1}{2 m}+1 +\f{\pi_2}{\f{\pi_1 L_1}{2 m}-1})N_{t+1}=(\f{L_1}{2 m}+1 +\f{\pi_2}{\f{\pi_1 L_1}{2 m}-1})(1+g^{max})N_t=(1+g^{max})P_t
\ee
The growth rate of total population between periods is $\f{\pi_1 L_1}{2m}-1$. \qed \\

\textbf{Proposition 6.} When \textbf{Proposition 4.} holds, in our three-period overlapping generation model, there is very limited output per capita growth before the aggregate output level $Y_t$ big enough. The total aggregate output $Y_t$ level grows at approximate constant rate $g^{max}$ because of the constant population growth. 
\be  Y_{t+1}=N_{t+1} y_{t+1}=(1+g^{max}) N_t y_t=(1+g^{max}) Y_t  \ee
 \qed\\




\textbf{Proposition 7.} With the survival rate $\pi_1$ large enough or the preference for next generate $\beta$ small enough, the optimal number of off-springs will begin to decrease.\\

\textbf{Remark.} There are also trade off between the starting new business rate $q$ and giving birth to next generation. When $q$ continues to increase, a higher percentage of people tend to have less kids in order to gain enough energy to make a start-up business. This is what we observed from Japan's case 40 years ago and from China's case 20 years ago. However, $q$ cannot continue to growth forever since it becomes harder to succeed in new business when the proportion of younger generation continues to  shrink, which is the following.\\
\textbf{Proposition 8.} The $q$ could not continue to increase when fertility rate continue to decrease quickly.\\

\textbf{Remark.} When more people tend to select to have less kids in exchange for more innovation attempt, they shrink the younger cohorts' population and consequently decreases the opportunity of next generation's new business success ratio. This is because in an older-cohort-dominate society, younger generation could not gain adequate human capital for succeeding in startup business since most important positions have already been occupied by older-cohort. Younger cohort could be promoted very slowly in an aging (low fertility) society. As a result, he/she could not gain enough customer resources, management skills and experience to start up his own business very successfully. That is to say, sacrificing the quantity of off-springs in exchange for new business investment might decrease the new business investment opportunity for the next generation.  This lead to the following result.\\
\textbf{Proposition 9.} A dynamic equilibrium exists in this model.\\


The fertility and innovation pattern should be displayed as the following process. First, the fertility rate is really high and innovation interest grows very slowly. Then with steady innovation (new business) success probability increasing, people tend to reduce the fertility rate and investment in "start-up" business, which contribute to giant economic growth. When the fertility rate becomes significantly lower, the incentive for innovation investment becomes lower and fewer people are willing to make a "start-up" business, which will ruin the economic growth pattern and cause economic stagnation. Finally when the winning chance in "venture capital gambling"  becomes very lower and people tend to have more off-springs at that moment, which would lead to the increasing chance for next generation. There will be fluctuation when this model reaches the final equilibrium. 

We can conclude from this theoretical overlapping generation model that the low fertility rate is really harmful for long-term economic growth. The short-term effect of low fertility rate could be positive since the raising cost are reduced. The innovation path are discussed very detailed in the above-mentioned nine propositions. The inequality level, therefore, could be ambiguous since there should be expected large income dispersion in a innovation-driven economy while the income dispersion could be reduced under economy stagnation and innovation stagnation. However, it remains an open question whether we could treat the income difference between a successful innovation payoff and a regular paycheck as inequality. The marginal contribution of an innovation could be more worthy than the innovation payoff to the entrepreneur. In other word, these successful "founders of new business" deserve even more than what they receive from the stock market.


\subsection{Empirical Setup and Result}

\subsubsection{Data and Methodology}
After the theoretical work, in this section we would like to use the empirical approach to test whether our prediction is correct or not. I just collect the macro-level data from the world bank online data set. All the data are from 1960 to current year (2015) though only few countries supply the estimated data of 2015. There are altogether 215 countries or political entities we collect from the data set and the rest 34 are summarized region  statistical information. All the data are annual data since most developing and undeveloped countries could not provide more specific data.  We have collected most of the available indicators depicting the economics growth level, innovation and inequality level.  

We use aggregate fertility level, population percentile among different age to describe the population structure and wish to examine the influence of those independent variables on the real economics growth, innovation level and inequality. It unveils the relationship between the innovation level, inequality and distorted (inverted-triangle-shaped population pyramid) population structure, which is the most crucial contribution of this empirical research  that have not yet been discussed specifically in the existing literature.

Naturally, the first step is to explore how distorted population structure affect economics growth rate. We run the following regression.
\be 
GDPg^k_t=\beta_0+\beta_1 lf_{90}^k +\beta_2 elf_{90}^k+\beta_3 {lf}_t^k+\beta_4 {elf}_t^k+\beta_5 f_t^k+\Gamma X+e_t^k
\ee
where: \\
$GDPg_t^k$ is the GDP growth rate of  country $k$ in year $t$; \\
$lf_{90}^k$ is the indicating variable ($lf_{90}^k \in \{ 0, 1\}$) which shows whether country $k$ belongs to low fertility country in the year 1990 (if fertility  of country $k$ is less than 1.85 in 1990 then $lf_{90}^k=1$ otherwise $lf_{90}^k=0$); \\
$elf_{90}^k$ is the indicating variable ($elf_{90}^k \in \{ 0, 1\}$) which shows whether country $k$ belongs to extreme low fertility country in the year 1990 (if fertility  of country $k$ is less than 1.35 in 1990 then $elf_{90}^k=1$ otherwise $elf_{90}^k=0$); \\
$lf_t^k$ is the indicating variable ($I_{lf}^k \in \{ 0, 1\}$) which shows whether country $k$ belongs to low fertility country in the year t (if fertility  of country $k$ is less than 1.85 in year t then $I_{lf}^k=1$ otherwise $I_{lf}^k=0$); \\
$elf_t^k$ shows the indicating variable ($elf_t^k \in \{ 0, 1\}$) which shows whether country $k$ belongs to extreme low fertility country in the year t (if fertility  of country $k$ is less than 1.35 in year t then $I_{elf}^k=1$ otherwise $elf_t^k=0$); \\
$f_t^k$ represents the fertility level in the year $t$ of country $k$. \\
$X$ are vectors that control other independent variables that influence the economics growth rate such as region belongings and foreign direct investment.

\subsubsection{The effect on economics growth}
We summarize the empirical result in the following table. We cluster the data in countries/economics entities level. In column (1) and (2) we run the regression by using data from all 215 countries; in column (3) and (4) only major countries (OECD countries and some important emerging economics entities) are taken into account while in column (5) and (6) only OECD countries results are reported. At the same time, we also summarize the result using different time interval. It could be expected we should focus on the impact of more recent years. The main reason is that the data 20 years ago could be incomplete and the measuring methods those emerging countries use could be less convincing before the invention of modern information industry booming and further globalization. In column (1), (3), (5) we use all year data (1966 to 2015) while in column (2), (4), (6) we only report the more recent panel data (1996 to 2015), discussing the impact on the growth rate in the past two decades. The robust standard errors after clustering are generated to calculate p-values.

An obvious question is why we select fertility rate of year 1990 as the low fertility indicator. Firstly, the year 1990 could be regarded as the beginning of modern low-fertility research. Before that most scholars and international organization never considered that the world could experience severe low-fertility  problem in the future. A lot of open report (United Nations Population Fund 1985, Ford foundations 1980) predicts the world would experience sharp population increase in the mid $21^{st}$ century. They argue Chinese population will reach 3 billion and Indian population would go to 2.5 billion in 2050 and conclude there will be a disaster. Also, in the late 1980s and early 1990s many scholars in the United States were contemplating seriously that the GDP of Japan would exceed USA at the end of $20^{th}$ century. 
Currently, the former chief economist of World Bank Justin Y. Lin (2012) and Committee member of People Bank's of China Daokui Li(2008) predicted that China's GDP growth rate will maintain at $8 \%$ level in the next 30 years and the GDP will be $3 \sim 4$ times as United States in 2050. Justin Lin (2012) made such prediction according to the past experience of Japan and Korea. He claimed those countries could maintain $8\%$ GDP growth rate for another 30 years after GDP per capita had reached one-sixth of the United States' and consequently China would repeat the same procedure. However, the fatal weakness of above-mentioned conclusions are they fail to even take a look at the drastic population structure change and extreme low fertility of those countries (China 1.18, Japan 1.4, South Korea 1.30 in 2012), which would exert dominant influence on future economics growth.  They overestimated the population in a great extent.  In the recent 25 years many scholars (Makoto 2001, N Ogawa, A Mason, A Chawla, R Matsukura, 2009, RR Rindfuss, KB Guzzo, SP Morgan .2003) accomplished important research on the topic of low fertility rate. As a result, we select fertility rate of the year 1990 as a pivotal index to depict future age structure. Secondly,  our research data are from 1966 to 2015 while the year 1990 is exactly the midpoint. Also, it is exactly 25 years from 1990 to now, which could be considered as the length of one generation. 

The following two Propositions are obvious and easy to prove.\\
\textbf{Proposition A.} If the fertility is $\alpha$, then the population of each generation  will increase by $\f{\alpha}{2}$ assuming all children stay alive. If we consider the possibility of childhood death, the increasing ratio will be adjusted to  $\f{\alpha}{2.1}$.\\
\textbf{Proposition B.} If the the fertility is $\alpha$ ($\alpha<2$). It would take $\f{25 \ln 0.5}{\log \alpha-\log 2.1}$ years to decrease the population by $50\%$ without considering any internal or external migration.\\
According to \textbf{Proposition B.}, it would take 137 years for a country to shrink to half size if the long-term fertility rate is 1.86 and take only 40 years for a country under 1.35 long-term fertility rate. Consequently, the population outlook of east Asian countries could be very pessimistic.

From Table \ref{1}, we will see the low fertility  indicators of 1990 have significant negative effect on GDP growth rate in 5 of 6 models. Interestingly, we find the those countries where experienced extreme low fertility contributed statistically positively on growth rate. This is mainly because the those extreme low fertility countries are from Asia and contributed most part of economics growth during these period (the coefficients of Asia are significantly positive).

It seems beyond expectation low fertility could contribute negatively to aggregate GDP growth rate since there will be fewer total GDP under fewer population.  However, traditional economics model (Solow 1957, Romer 1986) predicts higher GDP per capita with less population. In order to test for this hypothesis, we use GDP per capita growth rate as dependent variable and report the following empirical results.

Generally speaking, low fertility contributes significantly negatively (95 $\%$ confidence level) to GDP per capita growth rate, which contradicts traditional economics growth rate. At the same time, we could not ignore that the influence of current extreme low fertility rate contributes positively to GDP per capita growth. This could be explained that people tend to be beneficial from reducing cost of raising kids right now. It reminds us that the current fertility could exert a delayed effect on economics indicators.  



We define economics stagnation indicator by using following formula.
\be  I_{stagnation}=1 \ee if GDP per capita in 2014 $< 1.1*$ GDP per capita in 1994.
Otherwise  \be  I_{stagnation}=0 \ee We use Logit model, Probit model and Linear Probability Model to test the influence on economic stagnation. 
{

            &      (4.13)         &      (4.07)         &      (3.97)         \\

}

All models predict the probability of getting stuck in economic stagnation is higher under low fertility rate and extreme low fertility rate, which give us the same result with previous general model approach.

The impact on inequality level is ambiguous. Some coefficients are significantly positive and others are negative. We could notice that region influence dominates the fertility impact. For example, Latin America and Asian dummies contribute positive effect on GINI index while Europe dummy is negative. We can conclude that low-fertility influence are less important than region difference when we discuss the economics inequality level. Another possible explanation is that GINI index tend to estimate the difference between the rich and the poor while low-fertility would only have effect on between generation income difference. That is to say, it should have effect on the income inequality between the old and young but not between the rich and the poor. It deserves valuable future research.

Table \ref{4} to table \ref{6}  provide us first-hand result on innovation level. We find that both low-fertility index in the year 1990 and low-fertility itself exert negative influence on export ratios and new business per capita ratios. The results listed in Table \ref{4} and Table \ref{5} supply solid support on the negative effect of low fertility rate on innovation level and economics vitality. In Table \ref{4} we will find that past low fertility indicators have significant negative impact on high-tech output while current low fertility indicators in Table \ref{5} have significantly negative impact on new business per capita.  It reminds us the low-fertility could have different time-variant impact on different aspect of social innovation. In Table \ref{6}, time to new business could be considered as difficulty level of initiate a start-up or in a certain extent should be treated as difficulty to innovation.
Interestingly, in Table \ref{6} we find that current low-fertility could have raise such  difficulty while past low-fertility could reduce it. Again, it gives us strong hint we have to treat the low-fertility effect in a longer time period and focus on  the long-term dynamic effects. 

Now we define the innovation stagnation dummy under the following formula.
\be I_{innovation \ stagnation}=1 \ee if new business per capita in 2014 $<1.2*$ new business per capita in 1994.
Otherwise \be I_{innovation\  stagnation}=0 \ee

We use Logit model, Probit model and Linear Probability Model to test the influence
on innovation stagnation.

All three models show the probability of getting stagnant in innovation is increasing under low fertility rate while the probability is decreasing under extreme low fertility. The first part result is not surprisingly while the second part result is beyond further investigation with more specific micro level data.
\end{subsection}

\begin{subsection} {The effect on other important factors}


Generally OLG model predicts aging economy would increase tax ratio to support its pension system. No matter whether the pension system is Pay-as-you-go or fully-funded, the essence is to use next generation's work to support the old one. It could be expected with fewer next generation under low fertility, the tax ratio should increase. In the next table we test the effect on tax ratio over the GDP.

Even theoretical part does not depict the government's transfer payment. We are still interested the impact of low fertility on the tax rate.


Not surprisingly, past extreme low fertility leads to increasing tax rate, particularly in OECD countries. At the same time, the influence of current low fertility rate on tax ratio is negative. This could be explained by past low fertility rate could lead to current high dependency ratio, which directly impacts the tax collected in the pension and medical welfare system.  On the contrary, current low fertility rate could cut the cost of government transferred expenditure on childcare. Consequently the coefficients in column (1) and (2) are statistically negative. Generally speaking, the pension expenditure expense increase outweighs the childcare expense decrease so that the long-term overall effect on tax ratio is positive, which is shown in the row (5), particularly in OECD countries.

Table \ref{8} and Table \ref{9} report the effect of low fertility on long-term unemployment rate on overall population group and  female group respectively. We conclude that low-fertility rate have long-term negative effect on employment, particularly on female cohort. This result is quite counter-intuitive since it is generally believed that a female person is more able to find a job if her expected fertility rate is low. This result provides us hint that low fertility may increase job participating ratio of female and increase the female labor supply. Therefore, the long-term effect on female unemployment rate is positive.

Table \ref{8} and Table \ref{9} discuss the impact on unemployment rate. The long-run Philips Curve predicts that unemployment rate has no relationship with inflation rate while short-run Philips Curve predicts that those two variables are negatively correlated. Under Table \ref{10} the impact of low fertility on inflation rate is examined.

\subsection{Robustness Check and Discussion}

\subsubsection{Considering a more accurate definition of fertility rate}
}
Our definition of fertility rate is the actually total fertility rate among all population group. But in personal 
selection part only adult people make rational choice between "producing" more next generation, or supplying more labor work or going for a start-up.
In this case, the teenagers who are not old enough to enter the market should not be considered into the model. In other word, the adolescent fertility rate should be subtracted from the total fertility rate to make the depiction more precise. We revise our data set by the following formula.\\
\begin{centering}
 \textbf{adjusted-fertility rate=total fertility rate- adolescent fertility rate(births per 1000 women ages 15-19)/1000}
\end{centering}\\ \\
We use the re-calibrated fertility data to re-estimate our model. The following three tables show the difference between our original estimation and new estimation. (First table is the re-estimation of the effect on GDP per capita growth rate; the second table is on GINI index ; the third table is on new business per capita)


{

From Table \ref{A1}, Table \ref{A2} and Table \ref{A3}, we can see clearly that the t statistics become smaller. But most of the difference are minor so that
our conclusion made won't be changed in the previous section. 

\begin{subsection}{Considering economic cycling influence}
In this section we wish to take into account the economic cycling influence on the variables to re-estimate the model, which 
is a kind of robustness test. I have particular interest to check if low fertility rate hurts the economics indicators during recessions.

We find that US experience economics recession during 1970-1971, 1974-1975, 1980-1983, 1990-1991, 2001-2002, 2008-2009.
We use US economic downturn as an index to depict global economics condition. We re-estimate our model during the non-recession period and
recession period separately to see the difference with our original results. 

The following three tables show the difference between our original estimation and new estimation. (First table is the re-estimation of the effect on GDP per capita growth rate; the second table is on GINI index ; the third table is on new business per capita)

From Table \ref{B1}, Table \ref{B2} and Table \ref{B3}, we find that the impact of low-fertility rate during non-recession period become less significant while 
those impact become more significant during recession period. In other word, the low-fertility effect will move onto a more influencing stage when the global economics condition are not very good. The robustness check I finish here leaves an open door for future more valuable research.

\subsubsection{Re-estimating the effect on inflation by using a Probit model approach
}
In the previous section we conclude that the low-fertility effect on inflation is ambiguous. In this section we would like to separate the inflation index into
different categories and reestimate the effect.

The inflation status could be divided into four different categories: 
\\(1) deflation: with inflation $< 0\%$ 
\\(2) low inflation: with inflation $\in [0\%, 3\%]$ 
\\(3) moderate inflation with inflation $\in [3\%, 10\%]$ 
\\(4) high inflation with inflation $> 10 \%$ 

Table \ref{C1}  to Table \ref{C4} reports the Probit estimation of the low-fertility effect on the probability of having deflation, low inflation , moderate inflation and high inflation respectively.

{

By dividing the inflation into four different categories, we can conclude low-fertility rate will reduce the chance of becoming deflation or 
high inflation. Meanwhile, it can increase the probability of entering low inflation range quite significantly, particularly if we use more recent data.
Perhaps this is the result of oil rocketing in the 1980s which are not taken into account if we use more recent data.
The effect on entering moderate inflation range is somehow ambiguous. Low fertility could decrease the chance of entering moderate inflation range while extreme low fertility increase. We conclude that the low fertility does decrease the volatility of inflation range by reducing the chance of entering deflation or high inflation range. Most countries with low fertility rate and extreme low fertility rate enjoy low or moderate inflation, which definitely is a good news for economic stability.

\subsection{Concluding Remarks}

Recent studies  show low-fertility rate has a long-term negative effect on economics growth, particularly by studying the case of Japan and European countries (K Christensen, G Doblhammer, R Rau, JW Vaupel, 2009). Most literature would argue low fertility rate would damage economics growth in the long run because of the insufficient labor supply and demand. The main contribution of this paper is that it first unveils the fact low fertility rate would provide negative incentive to innovation and venture capital attempt in the long run, which could discourage the entrepreneurship and new invention across generation. Taking Japan as an example, thanks to the baby boomer policy after the Second World War, when these baby boomers entered the job market in the 1970s, there were a lot of innovative new business during that period. People were willing to attempt high-risky start-ups and  new techniques in a younger-cohort-dominant society, which paved the way for Japan‘s economic take-off during that period. However, when the fertility rate becomes extremely low and the whole economy is dominated by the old cohort, younger people could not gain enough human capital to win  risky pioneering business gambling, which leads to fewer innovation after 1990s. We can see the fact that all the world-famous companies in Japan are still those companies that have already gained their reputation thirties years ago. The "lost 20 years of Japan" is mainly because they gave too little birth to the next generation 40 years ago that wiped out the innovation potential for the next generation. People could not accuse the younger cohort don't work hard nowadays to catch up their American peers. Actually younger workers in Japan work much more diligent than their American peers but having inadequate human capital to make start-ups like Google, Facebook and Tesla. The picture was the situation for Japan for the last twenty years and is the case for South Korean currently and will be the situation of China in the future, where these three countries are all suffering from extremely low fertility rate dilemma but on different stage.

We conclude this paper by claiming that low fertility rate would not only reduce the GDP growth rate but also the GDP per capital rate in the long run, which is on the opposite side of general belief that lowing population could enhance the per capita growth rate. It is mainly because younger cohort could have little chance to win pioneer business gambling in the aging economy. There will be little innovation and new business in the long run. In contrast of general belief, our empirical result shows low fertility will increase long-term female unemployment rate, which indicates there should not be so many female workers participating the labor market. The volatility of interest rate and inequality rate could be reduced, which could be potential evidence of stagnant economics phenomenon. All the countries suffering from the low fertility should encourage giving birth to more off-springs by providing enough subsidies. Also, this paper gives us hint the government should never let the society dominated by the old cohort. An executable plan is Singapore's mode to reduce the old cohort's retirement pension, welfare and lower the decision power of the old cohort. Younger generation should have more chance to be in the high-ranking position, accumulating human capital for new innovation and new business, which is the long term engine of economics booming. Also, it is very important for female to give adequate birth to the next generation, which, in a certain extent, more crucial than how many work they contribute at this moment. Just as what Lee Kuan Yew, the founding father of Singapore told a female Ph.D. student is the year 2009 . "\textit{You should find a husband and give birth to next generation before 30. That is more important than your Ph.D. degree.}", the low fertility problem is the fatal reason for long term economic stagnation. An open topic is how we can get rid of the low fertility dilemma and it deserves future work, particularly in Asian countries.

\subsection{Appendix}
\textbf{Proof of Proposition 1}\\
Notice under the definition of $q_t$ we have
\be B_t=\sum_{k=1}^{N_t} b_k=qN_t (L_2-m n_t) =q N_t \tilde{L_2} \ee
We should maximize equation (\ref{3}) by choose $q$:
\be \max_q  A(q N_t \tilde{L_2})^\alpha (1-q) \tilde{L_2}+pA [q N_t \tilde{L_2}]^\gamma q \tilde{L_2}  \ee
which is equivalent to 
\be \max_q q^{\alpha} (N_t \tilde{L_2})^{\alpha-\gamma}(1-q) +pq^{\gamma+1} \ee
First Order Condition:
\be 
-(N_t \tilde{L_2})^{\alpha-\gamma} q^\alpha+(N_t \tilde{L_2})^{\alpha-\gamma} (1-q) q^{\alpha-1} \alpha +p q^\gamma (1+\gamma)=0
\ee
We will find q is uniquely depended by p with the following relationship.
\be  
p=\f{(N_t \tilde{L_2})^{\alpha-\gamma} (q-\alpha+q\alpha)}{(1+\gamma) q^{1+\gamma-\alpha}}
\ee
Since $1>>\gamma>>\alpha$  We can reduce it approximately into: 
\be p=(N_t \tilde{L_2})^{\alpha-\gamma}\f{1}{1+\gamma} (1+\alpha-\f{\alpha}{q}) \ee
which gives us the following expression:
\be 
q=\f{\alpha}{1+\alpha-(1+\gamma)p (N_t \tilde{L_2})^{\gamma-\alpha}} \label{*}
\ee
Clearly we have $\f{\p q}{\p p}>0$ \qed\\


\textbf{Proof of Proposition 3} Recall equation (\ref{*}), what we have is $\f{\p q}{\p N_t} > 0 $ and  $\f{\p q}{\p \tilde{L_2}}>0 $. Combining to $N_t=N_{t-1} E[n_{t-1}]$ and $E(\tilde{L_2})=L_2-m E[n_t]$ We could have $\f{\p q}{\p E[n_{t-1}]}>0 $ and  $\f{\p q}{\p E[n_{t-1}]}< 0 $,where $E[.]$ denotes expectation signals.  \qed\\



\textbf{Proof of Proposition 4} We evaluate $\theta_t$, $x_t$, $n_t$ near the steady state. We will have
\be 
\begin{split}
U= & \max_n \f{1}{1-\sigma}[(\theta y_t)^{1-\sigma}-1]
\\& +\f{1}{1-\sigma}\beta \pi_1 \f{1}{1-\sigma} [((1-\f{1}{2}n\theta-\pi_2 x) y_t)^{1-\sigma}-1]
\\&+\f{1}{1-\sigma} \beta^2 \pi_1 \pi_2 [(\f{1}{2}x \pi_1 y_t)^{1-\sigma}-1]
\end{split}
\ee
and
\be \f{\p U}{\p n}= \beta \pi_1 [(1-\f{1}{2}n\theta)y_t]^{-\sigma}(-\f{1}{2}\theta y_t) +\beta^2 ]\pi_1 \pi_2 [\f{1}{2}x\pi_1 n y_t]^{-\sigma}(\f{1}{2}x \pi_1 y_t) \label{**} \ee
if 
\be  \beta>\f{(1-\f{1}{2}n\theta -\pi_2 x)^{-\sigma} \theta}{ \pi_2 (\f{1}{2} x \pi_1 n)^{-\sigma} x \pi_1} \ee
We will have $\f{\p U}{\p n}>0$.\\
Together with the constraint $n_t m \leq L_1$, we have 
\be n^*=\f{L_1}{m} \ee \qed\\

\textbf{Proof of Proposition 7} Under formula (28), if 
\be \beta_1 \pi_1^{1-\sigma}>\f{(1-\f{1}{2} n \theta -\pi_2 x)^{-\sigma}\theta}{\pi_2 (\f{1}{2}x n)^{-\sigma} x} \ee
the \be \f{\p U}{\p n}<0 \ee
which means the fertility rate will drop from $\f{L_1}{m}$. Also in this case the population growth rate will drop from $g^{max}$ \qed\\

\textbf{Proof of Proposition 8} Since $\f{\p {q_t}}{\p p_t}>0$. 
\be I_{q_{t+1}>q_t}= I_{p_{t+1}>p_{t}} \ee
Define $1+g_t=G_t>1$ is the population growth rate for period t. From the definition of $p_t$ we have:
\be 
\f{p_{t+1}}{p_t}=\f{p(Y_{t+1})}{p(Y_t)}*\f{\f{G_{t-1}G_t}{G_{t-1}G_t+G_{t-1}}}{\f{G_{t-1}}{G_t+1}}=\f{p(Y_{t+1})}{p(Y_t)}*\f{G_t}{G_{t-1}} \approx (1+p'())*\f{G_t}{G_{t-1}} =(1+\epsilon)*\f{G_t}{G_{t-1}}
\ee
 when 
\be \f{G_t}{G_{t-1}}<\f{1}{1+\epsilon} \ee 
the $p_{t+1}$ would decreases. 
And according \textbf{Proposition 1.} $q_{t+1}$ will also decrease. \qed\\

\textbf{Proof of Proposition 9} Let the relationship between $q_{t+1}$ and $q_t$ as a function T: $q_{t+1}=T(q_t)$. T is decided by setting all first order condition of equation (1) to 0. 
Since all the first order condition are continuous and differentiable, it could be seen that T constructs a Banach Space. $T: [0,1] \to [0,1]$. According to  
\textbf{Proposition 8.} it is convex, compact and has a sup norm. Therefore the fixed point exists according to  Schauder Fixed Point Theorem. The unique fixed point is the dynamic equilibrium. \qed
