\end{section}




\begin{section} {Chapter 4: Income Inequality and Consumption Inequality in China}

\begin{subsection} {Introduction}
Thanks to the Reform and Opening-up Policy since 1978, the past three decades have witnessed dramatic economic growth in China with more than 10\% annualized growth rate (World Bank 2013). Accompanies with the economics prosperity magic, we could never fail to notice the alarming spike of inequality of China in the past 30 years. Measured by income Gini coefficient, the income inequality rose from 0.24 in 1991 to 0.33 in 2003 and 0.42 in 2010 (Cai, Chen and Zhou 2010; Ravallion & Chen 2004; Meng et al 2010; Li and Luo 2010). Hukou restriction and urbanization could serve as major explanatory power to the booming income inequality. All the income group experienced significant income raise while the top one enjoyed the fastest growth rate. Meanwhile, consumption inequality rose with similar magnitude. Consumption per capita Gini coefficient increased from 0.25 in 1992 to 0.33 in 2013.
The proportional income and consumption inequality growth points to the central question of this paper. According to basic economical principals and evidence in other economies, consumption inequality should be less than income     because of diminishing marginal consumption interest
(Katz 1992; Johnson and Smeeding 1998). Rich cohort tends to save more to partially insure against some permanent income shocks (Blundell, Pistaferri and Preston 2008). Precautionary saving motives and liquidity constraints help explain the excess marginal saving rate among the top-income cohort against uninsured idiosyncratic risk (Aiyaragi 1994). Empirical evidences also support the theoretical proposition for the United States (Beach 1977; Deaton 1992; Atkinson 1993; Wolfson 1994; Blundell 2008), Canada (Buse 1982; Pendakur 1998), United Kindom (Atkinson 1993), Netherlands and many other economic entities (Gouveia and Tavares 1995). But we could hardly find similar evidence for China. The reported consumption inequality is more than or approximately equivalent to the reported income inequality (Cai et al. 2010).
This article attempts to provide a feasible approach to solve the puzzle. Are the consumption habits among different income group in China different from those abovementioned developed economies? Or is it because the survey statistical data inaccurate to capture the true income and
consumption inequality? Our answer is the latter one. The income inequality in under-measured in the survey. Using our proposed methodology to compute the real income and consumption inequality, the income inequality outnumbers the consumption one in all available survey periods in China, which is consistent with the economic intuition and empirical results among other regions of the world.
To unveil the true income of all groups, we need to revisit and revise the inaccurate reported income and consumption data. For instance, high income cohort in China may underreport their aggregated income for tax evasion purpose. Individual in US has to report all income (labor income, capital gains, self-employed returns, inherited legacies, etc.) to IRS to tax return purpose annually while there is no need for individual in China to do so. Consequently, alternative income besides labor gains might not be reported to statistics bureau and survey investigators. At the same time, some illegal income sources would never be reported accurately, particularly for those rent-seekers. Communist Party members and government officials, to some extent, obtain increasing opportunities to get (unlawful) benefits from the rent-seeking economic activities like license-issuing and land-regulation. From the media report and Central Discipline Inspection Commission’s announcement, there are more than 120 high-level government officials founded to commit severe corruption crimes recently. The systematic underreporting income (Zhang, Giles, and Rozelle 2012; Gong, Liu and McLaughlin 2017) of Communist Party members leads to an unconvincing reported income data points and therefore distorts inequality measurement. So-called “gray-income” of high-income groups would be underestimated if we ignore the (unlawful) benefits of government affiliated or government related employees (Wang and Woo (2011)). The reported consumption inequality on average is 0.9\% to 12.6\% higher than income inequality between 1995 and 2002 while our imputed revealed (true) income inequality on average is 0.3\% to 26\% higher than consumption inequality for the same period, which solves the empirical puzzle between China and the rest of world raised by the existing literature.
The following part of the articles is presented as follows. Section 2 presents a true-income- revealing computational methodology by implementing Engel's law between necessity goods and aggregated consumption. Section 3 describes the data set and empirical findings of possible income underreporting problem for some cohorts, income and consumption inequality, provides comparison to existing inequality literature calculations and highlights our contributions. Section 4 provides concluding remarks and guidance to future research.

\end{subsection}

\subsection{True Income Revealing Methodology: The Engel's law between necessity goods consumption and income}


Engel’s Law captures the empirical relationship between necessity goods consumption and aggregate consumption. It is well documented that as income rises, the proportion of income spent on
even if absolute amount rises. We want to use the well-establish necessity goods consumption proportion and income relationship to recover the true income of all individuals. We have the following Equation(1).
\begin{equation*}
    w_{ij} = \beta_0 + \beta_1 log(I_{ij}) + \PSi X_{ij} + \gamma_j +u_i
\end{equation*}

where $w_{ij}$ denoted necessity goods consumption proportion for family i in city j and $I_{ij}$  represents income for this household. $u_i$ are the idiosyncratic error terms.

$X_{ij}$ here denotes certain family characteristics influencing necessity goods consumption share, which includes family size, in-house sex ratio, number of children aged 15 or less, average age and age squared
of family members, age and age squared of household head, education level of household heads, gender of household heads, number of refrigerators, house size, independency of kitchen and locations of house. $\gamma_j$ captures city fixed effect that controls local price levels.

The definition of consumption and income could vary under different criteria. We would try three approaches by varying the categories of the necessity goods.

(a) Food consumption approach

(a1) $I_{ij}$ includes all wage income, net business income, net property income and net transfer income.Food consumption includes cigarette consumption.

(a2) $I_{ij}$ contains all wage income, net business income, net property income, transfer income, in-kind income and rent benefits from house rental (market rent minus rent paid). Cigarette consumption is not included in the food consumption.

For house renters, we impute the rental house benefits as the market rent of the occupied housing . For house owners, the rental house benefits are
calculated as market rent minus rent paid. Both results are reported for the main results and robustness check parts.
The empirical exercises are based on two important assumptions. (i) We assume that the food consumption share is accurate for all people. Underreporting income does not necessitate underreporting food consumption share in the survey. The latter one has no tax gains or utility obtaining. (ii) Income is accurately reported for the selected base group. As a result, equation (1) depicts the precise relationship between food share and income. It should be expected that coefficient $\beta_1$ is significantly negative to reflect the spirit of Engel’s Law.

Base group should be selected very carefully to fulfill the requirement of (b2) to avoid the potential bias. In other words, individuals in the base group should not either underreport or over-report their aggregated income. We adopt the following several approaches.
(X1) Base group is the bottom X per cent income group.
(X2) Base group is the bottom X per cent income group with the all family members not belonging to Communist Party.
We will let X vary from bottom 10\%, 11\%, ..., 40\% to report 31 different estimate results in Table 3 for the year 1995, 1999, 2002 respectively to guarantee robustness. The reason for choosing (X1) as base group is that low income cohort has less tendency to report the income inaccurately. They don’t need to underreport the income for the tax evasion purpose. On the contrary, they have very limited incentive to over-report income for the government subsidy as Chinese governmental subsidy and transfer payment to the poor is of vast scarcity during 1995 and 2002. We could notice (X2) is a subset of (X1). (X2) is expected to serve a better base group since non-communist party members have the least opportunities touching the “grey income” territory and therefore the income reports tend to become more convincing. The only differences between base group (X1) and (X2) is the low income communist party member. There is empirical evidence (Gong, Liu and McLaughlin 2017) documenting potential “grey- income” opportunities for those low income party members. For instance, party members may have the chances to receive gift cards and food subsidy during holiday seasons. It is reported many low-level communist party employees and party members of state-own enterprises misuse the reimbursement policy to pay personal items and travel expenditures with public resources. Thus, (X2) serves another alternative approach to unveil the true income for the whole universe.

Also, the following two base group selection approaches are also implemented for robustness check to increase the exercise validity.

(X3) Base group is the bottom X per cent income group with the all family members neither belonging to Communist Party nor working in the governmental institutions or state-owned enterprises.

(X4) Base group is the bottom X per cent income group with the all family members not belonging to Communist Party. And all the base group members should work in the companies. (Here companies represent all types of corporations including state-owned, private-owned and individual owned enterprises. Government related institutions are excluded)

Notice the base group in (X3) and (X4) are subsets of the one in (X2). Despite the strength of more precise selection of the base group, (X3) and (X4) could lead to the potential weakness of more scarce data points in the base group. Comparing to (X2), (X3) excludes the low income party members in the non-communist party dominated corporations, with little evidence in the existing literature documenting potential “grey income” opportunities to unlawfully taking advantage of party memberships. Non- communist party dominated corporations are widely believed to be profit maximizing economy entities, with sufficient incentive and well-recognized ability to prevent hazardous rent-seeking behaviors of the employees no matter the party memberships. (X4) relaxes a little bit by including all companies’ workers both state-owned and private-owned. With recent literature evidence, the similarity between state-owned enterprises (SOE) and private-owned ones outnumbers the one between SOE and governmental institutions (Song 2015), overturning the previously widely believed concept on the opposite direction. Therefore, we have reasonable evidence to put together both SOE and private-owned ones to further check the robustness of our examination.

From Chart 1, the food share among aggregated consumption drops from 50.1\% in 1995 to 42.1\% in 1999 and then 37.7
\% in 2002 with National Bureau of Statistics (NBS) official data, indicating the downward trending in the food consumption with economics development, with absolute consistency of the definition of necessity goods. Under our proposed (a1) definition of food consumption, the food shares in consumption plummet from 47\% in 1995 to 43\% in 2002. While under (a2) definition food shares decrease from 49\% in 1995 to 40\% in 2002, reflecting an even sharper reduction in the cigarette consumption given cigarette is taken into (a2) definition. All the evidences give us hint that food consumption is appropriately chosen as the representative of necessity goods, with both theoretical foundation and empirical supports.

(b) Clothes consumption approach

Clothes is also treated as base consumption with income elasticity between zero and one. The cloth consumption will not likely be cut back on even when times are tough, sharing the similarity with food consumption. We also use two approaches to recalibrate the true income of the overall cohort. In (b1) the income includes all wage income, net business income, net property income and net transfer income and in (b2) in-kind income and rent benefits from house rental are also taken into account the income.
(X1) to (X4) approaches are implemented in the similar way of part (a) food Engel's Law estimated equation. The only difference is that in the control variables X01 we ignore number of children aged 15 or less, number of refrigerators, house size, independency of kitchen and locations of house but keep all the rest. Naturally those deleted control variables have little relationship with the household aggregated clothes consumption but only food consumption. In order to make the estimate results more precisely we make such deletion. All clothes consumption containing school uniform spending are incorporated. Unfortunately, we don’t have the official summary statistics of clothes consumption shares from BLS China. But still, in Chart 1, our calculation shows a 2\% drop of clothes shares from 12\% in 1995 to 10\% in 1999 and 2002 respectively no matter what kind of income definition we use. As a result, clothes consumption serve properly as a representing item of necessity good to reveal the true income for the whole cohort.

(c) Utilities consumptions approach

Utilities spending is generally treated as another crucial part of necessity goods spending. The adoption of utilities spending in our analysis is natural and displays supportive evidence to unveil the relationship between income inequality and consumption inequality. The definition of utility here includes water, electricity, fire coals spending. Natural gas is not listed separately in the survey data and not widely introduced and assembled in the vast majority families of China during the 1990s. We only report the 1995 and 1999 results as there are only less than 200 valid observations filling in utility consumption in 2002. (X1) to (X4) approached are repeated with similar methodologies documented in part (a). Similarly, in (c1) the income includes all wage income, net business income, net property income and net transfer income while in-kind income and rent benefits from house rental are included in (c2) approach. For the control variables X01 we also delete number of children aged 15 or less, number of refrigerators, house size, independency of kitchen and locations, which seem to uncorrelated with the household utility consumption behavior.


From Chart 1 we could find the utility shares increase from 4\% in 1995 to 7
\% in 2002, somehow dis-according with the observed phenomenon of decreasing clothes and utility shares
plummet. Nevertheless, we still list the utility approach to enhance the validity of our examination as utility, regardless of standards, is regarded as the indispensable part of necessity goods consumption.

We have to ask if there exist any other particular consumption variables that are also appropriate to estimate the Engel's law relationship. For example, Ding, He and Zhu (2017) use more general durable and non-durable goods to reveal the true income level following the econometric methodology of Aguiar and Bils (2015). With our empirical approaches there must not exist structural break of consumption habits between base group and non-base group. For instance, the non-durable goods consumption habits might vary very differently for the base group (poorer cohort) and the non-base group (the rest). As a result, we might feel uncomfortable using non-durable goods consumption behaviors to reveal the true income inequality in this paper. Also, Engel's Law literally captures only the linear relationship between necessity good consumption share and aggregated income, which is inappropriate to describe other non- linear relationship. Consequently, we are not using variables like education expenditure share that might have complex relationship with earnings as Mincer’s Equation reminds us possible linear and quadratic relationships between education and earnings. Food, clothes and utility are thus by far all the possible variables in CHIP database we could use to estimate the Engel's Law linear relationship.

In the last part of this section we briefly introduce how to recover each person’s aggregate income through Engel's Law estimations. It is straightforward to reverse Equation (1) to impute the realized income but it could suffer from the omitted variable bias problem. As a result, we would like to adopt the alternative imputation method introduced by Pissaride, Weber 1989 and Deng, Wei, Wu 2016.

Household true income is decomposed into two separate components: reported and unreported parts. By assuming unreported parts are proportional to the reported income. We have the following relationship between those two parts of income. We have the following expression (2).

\begin{equation*}
    I_i = RI_i + UI_i = RI_i + \mu RI_i = RI_i (1 + \mu)
\end{equation*}

where I is true income, RI is reported income and UI is underreported income. Replacing the decomposed income expression (2) in the basic Engel's Law Equation (1), the following expression (3) is obtained for all individuals.

\begin{equation*}
    w_{ij} = \gamma_0 + \gamma_1 \log (RI_{ij}) + \gamma_2 \log (1+\mu) + \Psi X_{ij} + \gamma_j + u_i
\end{equation*}

If we assume only people not in the base group might inaccurately report overall earnings, the proportion of reported income can be estimated with a dummy variable indicating whether in the base group. We have the following equation (4).

\begin{equation*}
    w_{ij} = \gamma_0 + \gamma_1 \log (RI_{ij}) + \gamma'_2 dummy_i + \Psi X_{ij} + \gamma_j + u_i
\end{equation*}

where dummy equals 0 referring base group and 1 indicating comparison group member i, respectively.

Comparing Equation (3) and (4) we have the identical relation $\gamma'_2 dummy_i = \gamma_2 $ so that we could estimate $\mu$, the proportion of underreported income to reported income as $\mu = exp(\frac{\gamma'_2}{\gamma_2}) - 1$.  The final income inequality and consumption inequality results are reported under equation (3) and (4). We do notice there are other income inequality measurements like 20:20 ratio, Palma ratio, Hoover index, coefficients of variation, Theil index, Atkinson Index, etc. To fairly compare with the results from the vast majority literatures of income and consumption, only income and consumption Gini index are calculated, reported and compared.

\subsection{Data and results}

The data we use in the analysis is from Chinese Household Income Project Series (CHIP). The data is originally designed to measure and estimate the distribution of personal income in both rural and urban areas of the People's Republic of China. Data is collected in 1988, 1995, 1999, 2002, 2007 and 2013. These surveys were carried out as part of a collaborative research project on incomes and inequality in China organized by Chinese and international researchers, with assistance from the National Bureau of Statistics. For the purpose of studying income inequality and consumption inequality, we only use CHIP year 1995, 1999 and 2002 data to accomplish the empirical analysis.

CHIP 1988 is not taken into account since it only surveys household basic consumption expenditures like food, rental houses, fuel, transportation and medical treatment. Many other crucial items like expenditure on home equipment, facilities and services, miscellaneous goods and services are not reported, being inconsistent with the survey data in year 1995, 1999 and 2002. As a result, we are not able to obtain the true aggregated consumption data and necessity goods expenditure shares, in particular for the base group cohort. Equation (1) can therefore never be estimated appropriately to reveal the true income situation for other percentile groups. CHIP 1995, 1999 and 2002 implement consistent survey methodologies and data cleaning, serving the appropriate purpose to calculate food, clothes and utility expenditure shares. CHIP 2007 and 2013, however, fail to include two kinds of household in-kind benefits: (1) the discrepancies between paid rents and market rental prices for home rents. (2) The differences between paid mortgage interests for home owners. The failure of including in-kind benefits would lead inaccurate aggregated income for the base group (Li 2010) so that we could not step further to estimate the true relationship between the necessity goods expenditure and aggregated income. The inconsistency in the CHIP 2007, 2013 comparing to CHIP 1995, 1999, 2002 does not permit us to calculate income defined in (a2). Overall speaking, CHIP 1995, 1999, 2002 are the longest available dataset to allow us to compare time series income inequality and consumption inequality trend consistently and convincingly.

Table I depicts the summary statistics of CHIP reported income inequality and consumption inequality from 1995 to 2002. Column 2 reports income Gini (under the income definition in (a1)); column 3 reports an alternative income Gini (under the income definition in (a2)); and column 3 documents consumption Gini coefficient (including cigarettes expenditures). Overall speaking, both income inequality and consumption inequality increase slightly during the turn of the millennium. The income inequality taking into account the in-kind benefits (a2) rises approximately 5.5 per cent while under the traditional definition it hikes almost 14.7 per cent. In accordance with Li (2010), (a2) provides a better measurement of aggregated household than (a1). It is not difficult for us to discover that the income inequality does not boost significantly during this period by adopting more accurately measured income statistics. Meanwhile, we could never fail to notice the significant gap between (a1) and (a2), particularly for the year 1995. The housing marketization reform during the last five years of twentieth century could serves a powerful explanatory power for the major discrepancies between different income Gini results. Due to the continuous housing market reform policy, the actual paid housing expenditures converge to the market housing prices, eliminating gradually the in-kind benefits, particularly for the rich cohort. Therefore, the astounding income Gini differences measured under different criteria in 1995 should be explained by underreported in-kind benefits for those who benefited from planned economy housing policy.

The reported income inequality level is slightly still less than consumption Gini, which arises the central topic of our paper. We compare the literature documented income inequality from various datasets in Table 2. Yue, Li (2007) reported 0.363 and 0.368 income Gini for year 1995 and 2002, respective, in which outnumber the corresponding consumption Gini for those two years. Except Yue, Li (2007), the vast majority research documented the income Gini is less than the consumption Gini with various micro- level data sources. (Meng (2005), Jin, Li, Wu (2010), Meng (2004), Khan and Riskin (2001), Benjamin, Brandt and Giles (2005), Cai, Chen, Zhou (2010)). The following part reports our Engel's Law results, attempting to provide true relationship between income inequality and consumption inequality in China and supplying one possible unreported “grey income” estimate for certain cohort in China.

Table 3 reports the Engel's Law results from food consumption. The independent variable income is adjusted based on Consumer Price Index (CPI) published by National Bureau of Statistics. First the Engel's Law of inverse correlation between food share and aggregated income is indeed confirmed that the food share would drop by 0.08\% to 0.12\% in 1995, 0.06\% to 0.09\% in 1999, and approximately 0.10\% respectively if income level raises 1\%. Generally speaking, the high income group significantly underreports aggregated income per se. For example, if we take bottom 30\% as base group, upper income cohort (the rest 70\% in the survey data) on the average has 0.74\% less food consumption share (due to potential underreported aggregated income) under the income definition (a1) in 1995. While under the alternative definition (a2) taking into account the in-kind benefits, the drop of food consumption share for the high income cohort boosts to 1.85\% significantly in 1995. It gives us hint that the revealed income is even less reliable for the upper income group when more accurate measurement is considered to appropriately capture the overall income. When we look at the year 1999 result, upper income group on average responds 1.20\% below accurate food share without considering in-kind benefits while 1.03\% below adjusting under income definition (a2). For the year 2002, the upper income group seems to report the aggregated income more reliably. That said, the regression result generally does not indicate significant deviation of the revealed food share from the true one. We have to pay attention that if we only select bottom 40\% as base group, the reported income is even more distort. For instance, under such criteria, the upper income group significantly underreports food share (due to underreported income) by 2.6 per cent in 1995 and 3.3 per cent in 1999 under income measurement (a2). On the contrary, if we use bottom 10\% as base group, the rest 90\% on the average over reports 0.24\% in 1995; 0.28\% in 1999 and 0.01\% in 2002. We do not literally believe that using only bottom 10\% as base group could generate as convincing results as using 30\% approach since the survey data might not either record the aggregate income for the extreme poverty group accurately or appropriate measure their consumption proportion accurately. Meanwhile, a less populated base group does not provide us adequate confidence to appropriately measure the Engel's Law relationship. Generally speaking, the upper income group inaccurately reports aggregate income, particularly when we consider the in-kind benefits.

Table 4 shows the Engel curve estimation result from clothing consumption. Although clothes are traditionally believed to belong to the category of necessity goods, initial results show that clothing expenditures increase with respect to increase and hence indicate clothes luxury goods in China. Identity recognition for the rich cohort could serve as one possible explanation for increasing consumption on clothing. It could also be the case that employees in the state-owned agencies and corporations underreport the uniform benefits. The clothes share typically hikes 0.02\% to 0.05\% when income rises by each 1\% no matter what income and consumption criteria we adopt for all the available survey years. That said, the Engel's Law for the food and the clothes are not exactly in the same direction and consequently we have to explain the coefficient of upper income dummy in Table 4 in the alternative direction (comparing to Table 3). Again, if we take bottom 30 per cent as base group, upper income cohort significantly over reports clothes share (due to potential income underreporting) by 0.51\% taking into account in-kind benefits etc. (under measurement (b2)) in 1995, 0.11\% in 1999 and 0.06\% in 2002. By comparing column 2 and column 4, an increasing statistical significance is observed if we adopt a more appropriate income measurement (b2), which means the accuracy of reported income is even less convincing for the upper income cohort in the year 1995. The Engel's Law of clothing further reminds us the potential aggregated income underreporting for the rich cohort and thus the true income inequality might be more severe than it is reported directly from the surveys.


We repeat the exercise by segregating the bottom income base group from the rest with respect to utility consumption share in Table 5. Due to data availability we only report year 1995 and 1999 results with income measurement (c2). From the first and third column we could see that utility is empirically confirmed as traditional necessity good by the Engel's Law estimation. Generally speaking, utility share shrinks by 0.11 to 0.18 per cent when aggregated income rises 1 per cent in 1995 and 1999. Similar to Table 3, we could therefore explain the coefficients in column two and four in the similar way of elaborating food consumption share. The utility share is underreported by 0.29\% (significantly) in 1995 and 0.20\% in 1995 due to possible underreported income for the upper income cohort if we take bottom 30 per cent as base group. In short, looking through Table 3 to Table 5, the upper income group might suffer the inaccurately underreported income problem and lead to underestimate of the true income inequality for all the survey years.

able 6 to Table 8 reports the results of X2 approach (using low income non-party member households as base group) with respect to food, clothes and utility share Engel's Law relationship, respectively. In accordance with Li (2010), we only estimate the result with income involving in-kind benefits. In Table 6, we find that the food share is no longer diminishing with respect to aggregated income when considering party membership effect. Gong, Liu and Song (2017) documented significant underreported income and widely existing “grey income” phenomenon of communist party members. The changing sign of the coefficient of income could therefore be a direct result of potential dishonest income report of those party members. We should still notice that the coefficients of log income and upper income dummy variables are mostly positive in Table 6 while negative in Table 3. The same signs of both coefficients give us hint that upper income group, no matter whether taking into account the possible party membership effect, underreports the true income when we estimate the food share Engel's Law. The true income inequality outnumbers the reported one to some extent and we will discuss the details in the later part of the paper.

The result of clothes share estimation in Table 7 (X2 approach) does not differ very much from X1 approach in Table 3. The clothes consumption is still empirically considered to be luxury goods even considering party membership effect. The coefficients of income, upper income dummy variable remain significantly positive and (the same signs) indicate “grey income” possibility of the upper income cohort. Table 8 reports utility estimation results for the year 1995 and 1999, being quite consistent with the estimation results in Table 5. The utility consumption is not only theoretically in the domain of necessity goods but also empirically confirmed. The significant negative signs of income and upper income dummy hints us again the potential underreported income for the rich cohort.

Table 6 documents opposite signs of income and numbers of party members while Table 7 and Table 8 reports the same signs. In other words, food Engel's Law estimates possible income over-report of party members while clothes and utility Engel's Law provide party members’ underreporting estimation results. We need to pay closer attention to the consumption share habits of party members. The food Engel's Law results for the income party members could be misleading since quite decent amount of communists benefits from government subsidized and/or state-owned low-price cafeterias, being not included in the CHIP in-kind benefits consideration. Consequently, extra cautions must be paid when we attempt to illustrate the results in Table 6. As there is no literally documented solid evidence that party members benefit from utility subsidizing, the estimation results in Table 8, to some extent, provide more reliable estimation.

Table 9 to Table 11 record estimation results under approach (X3) for food, clothes and utility share Engel's Law, respectively. Base group here is the bottom X per cent (X varies from 10\% to 40\%) income group with the all family members neither belonging to Communist Party nor working in the governmental institutions or state-owned enterprises. Interestingly, after controlling effects of household working in all government sectors, government institutions, state and local government owned companies, urban collective institutions and state (shared) owned companies, only utility displays necessity good characteristics with diminishing marginal consumption share. However, the coefficients of income and upper income dummy all have the same sign (positive in food share Table 9; negative in clothes share Table 10 and utility share Table 11), indicating possible underreported income for the upper income cohort even after controlling working sectors in government related institutions or corporations. Notably with the coefficients in state owned companies all statistically significant (positive in food share Table 9; negative in clothes share Table 10 and utility share Table 11), the party membership effect in approach (X2) could be more precisely explained by state government owned companies’ effect. Meanwhile most coefficients of local government owned companies have the same significant signs with the coefficients of income. We could make the statement that employees in local government owned companies also have possible unreported income (except the estimation results from the utility share Table 11). Most coefficients of government institutions and urban collective institutions are insignificant. There does not exist significant over-reporting or underreporting income of the members from those sectors in our (X3) empirical analysis results. Generally speaking, controlling effects of household working in state or local government related institutions and companies could generate more detailed and precise estimation of whom may inaccurately report the aggregated income in the survey. From Table 9 to Table 11 we could make the rough statement that upper income class, employees from state and local government owned companies should have had undocumented “grey income” from the consumption habits. The direct calculation of income Gini coefficients from the reported income data might lead to unconvincing conclusion. Most Engel's Law equation estimations point to downward bias of income inequality and it would be adjusted in the next sector to fairly compare the true relationship 
between income inequality and consumption inequality.

Relaxing the base group restrictions of (X3) a little bit, Table 12 to Table 14 provides robustness check results of approach (X4) using low income, non-party workers in all types of companies, reporting Engel equation estimations with respect to food, clothes and utility shares. We have more base observed base group data points in approach (X4) to check the validity and robustness of our estimation results in approach (X3). We do not observe significant differences between the results of (X3) and (X4). Food and clothes consumption are empirically treated as luxury goods with increasing share to income hike while utility is still considered to be necessity goods according to the regression results. Relaxing the range of base group predicts downward biased responded income for the upper income cohort as well. Some of the estimation results (Food 1995) shows party members over-report income while others (Clothes 1995, Clothes 2002) direct the opposite allegation. It is uncertain whether workers in the government sectors would over-report or underreport their aggregate income since the results from food Engel's Law (Table 12) tell the different story from clothes Engel's Law (Table 35). Dividing the occupation more meticulously does generate more detailed and precise estimation if we compare regression coefficients of (X3) and (X4). Nevertheless, robustness checks (X4) defend the validity of using non-party, non-party-dominated low income employees as base group. Most conclusions (such as high-income cohort underreports earnings) remain very similar.


From Table 3 to Table 14 we report most of the estimation results by letting bottom X\% income vary from 10 to 40 per cent. The empirical estimation results do not differ significantly when X is in the range of 20 to 40. It indeed, however, generates a little bit different results if we use only bottom 10 per cent income cohort as base group. The base group data is the scarcest if letting X = 10\% and therefore we are least confident to use estimation results from bottom 10\%. In other word, using bottom 30 or 40 per cent might provide us more reliable and convincing results.

From the empirical exercise (X1) to (X4), people with high income, working in the state/local government owned corporations tend to inaccurately respond to the CHIP survey (typically downward biased). We use Equation (3) and (4) to recover the true aggregate income for each person. The revealed
income inequality and consumption inequality are summarized in Table 15 by assuming reliable surveyed food, clothes and utility consumption share in CHIP 1995, 1999 and 2002.

The overall income inequality is the smallest if not considering in-kind benefits. The average Gini coefficients are 0.282, 0.335 and 0.333 for the year 1995, 1999 and 2002 respectively (approach X1). However, if we treat the definition of income more precise to use the aggregated income including in- kind benefits. The Gini coefficients raise to 0.338, 0.339 and 0.344 respectively for those three years. Adjusting for the possible Communist Party members’ underreporting problem would further boost the income Gini approximately 10 more per cent, say 0.357, 0.374 and 0.377 in 1995, 1999 and 2002. Finally, if the occupation base reported inaccuracy is also taken into account and adjusted according to Equation (3) and (4), the overall income inequality should rise to 0.379, 0.397 and 0.409 for those three years, which is quite astonishing considering the level hiked from the original reported data points. These are the summary of the inequality results we imputed on the high level. If we look at the more detailed separate results, thee estimated inequality from clothes Engel's Law estimation is the highest while from utility remains the lowest. From Table 3 to Table 14, utility consumption is always empirically demonstrated to be necessity good no matter whether we control the high-income dummy, communist party numbers, occupational conditions, with diminishing marginal consumption interests. On the contrary, food and clothes consumption occasionally display the characteristics of luxury goods. Therefore, it could be claimed that estimation from utility Engel's Law provides the most conservative estimation for the income inequality throughout the time period.


Looking at the overall trend of income inequality, the increasing levels (2-3\%) of income inequality from 1995 to 1999 and 1999 to 2002 are almost proportional to the boosting levels of consumption inequality. Somehow it is quite inconsistent with the literature (Cai, Chen, Zhou 2010) arguing alarming spike (8-10\%) of income inequality. We argue that the income inequality is already very high before Chinese housing marketization reform in 1998 if considering the in-kind benefits. If the possible report inaccuracy is adjusted for high income, communist party members, state and local government owned company cohort, the income Gini is already over 0.4 line for all years (0.411 in 1995, 0.425 in 1999, 0.421 in 2002). It reminds us the large income inequality could be existing for very long period, not necessarily the results of swift economic reform and rapid economics development during 1990s. That is to say, the “grey income” for the high income, communist party members, state and local government owned company cohort could contribute significantly to the overall income inequality before and after the marketization reform is executed.


Table 16 summarizes our findings on income and consumption inequality and compares our results with the vast literatures. Even with our most conservative approach X1, the income inequality is higher than consumption inequality by 4.7 to 7.6 per cent. With the most detailed approach X3, the income inequality on average outnumbers the consumption one by 28.0 to 34.8 per cent. The income Gini from our most conservative approach X1 lies in the range of existing literatures while from X3 the inequality statistics are over most of the literatures’. For instance, the highest income Gini 0.450 in 2002 is estimated with NBS data by Bai, Zhang and Tang 2015, adjusting for the structural underreporting from various income sources. Our estimate for 2002 is between 0.344 and 0.421, taking the alternative approach adjusting the aggregated income from households with different occupations and party membership statuses. Other scholars (Ding, He and Zhu 2017; Wang and Li 2013) also use Engel Curve approach to estimate the relationship between true income and consumption inequality, but with different time period (2002-2007), different data sources (UHS). Their conclusion also differs us claiming that income inequality is still less consumption inequality, although being understated. Other income Gini estimates in the literatures are generally reported for the alternative purposes like measuring the urban- rural income gap, return to education and other public policies. For instance, the income Gini are reported as 0.240, 0.260 in 1995, 1999 with UHIES data (Meng 2005); 0.250, 0.280 in 1999, 2002 with CUHS data (Jin, Li, Wu 2010); 0.332 in 1995 with CASS data (Khan and Riskin 2001); 0.363, 0.368 in 1995, 2002 with CASS data (Yue, Li 2007); 0.370 in 1999 with RCRE data; 0.330 in 1999 with NBS data (Benjamin, Brandt and Giles 2005).

There could exist alternative structural or reduced form econometrics methodologies to unveil the true income distribution among the population. For instance, an Almost Ideal Demand System (AIDS) (Deaton and Meullbauer 1980) or its modification form (Aguiar and Bils 2013; Ding, He and Zhu 2017) could be used to precisely estimate the real income for individuals with various identity, occupations, locations, social statuses and income sources. However, the assumptions for those structural forms estimation could add extra complications and rely more data measuring techniques. In our Engel's Law estimation approach, only food, clothes and utility shares are assumed to be accurately reported for all individuals. It is not necessary to assume the absolute amounts of necessity goods consumptions are exactly documented. Meanwhile, the assumptions of approaches (X2), (X3) and (X4) are even weaker than the original Engel's Law approach (X1). For instance, in the approach (X3), we only need the income validity of the low income cohort with no government related influences. At the last step the robustness checks are examined to enhance the credibility of our empirical analysis.


Our analysis attempt to solve the puzzle of the literatures. We are educated that consumption inequality should be less than income inequality from both economic principles and empirical evidences in other countries. We are literally not sure if there exist behavioral deviation of the consumption habits of Chinese consumers from the traditional “rational agents”. This paper tends to provide an alternative explanation from a different angle. From our empirical results, we find the income inequality revealed from CHIP 1995 to 2002 should be at least 4.5 to 7.6 per cent higher than consumption inequality with the most conservative estimation. Other research like Wang and Li (2013) also adjusts the imputed income but fail to solve the income-consumption puzzle, claiming only less consumption inequality’s dominance over income inequality.


\subsection{Concluding Remarks}

Understanding income-consumption relationship is of vital importance in the territory of labor economics. We intend to provide preliminary evidence to defend the consistency of income-consumption relationship across the nation. At the same time, we contribute the existing income inequality literature an alternative empirical methodology to measure the true income inequality between 1995 and 2002 in China. On the high level, in imputed income inequality is 8.4\% to 31.8\% higher in 1995; 9.0\% to 36.5\% higher in 1999; 5.0\% to 28.4\% higher in 2002 than CHIP reported income inequality. And the true income inequalities dominate consumption ones by 7.5\% to 30.7\% in 1995; 7.6\% to 34.8\%; 4.7\% to 28.0\% in 2002. The empirical estimations could also help estimate the possible “gray income” of households benefited from communist party membership, occupational off-the-books opportunities. The most astonishing finding is that is income inequality in 1995 is already above 0.40 alarm line and remains pretty constant during the housing marketization reform and economics prosperity era. The usual statement is therefore short of empirical supports that increasing earnings inequality is the outcome of economic development. The summary statistics also show the importance of taking into account the in-kind benefits as part of aggregated earnings (Li 2010), which acts pivotally in the research of estimating income, calculating return to education, evaluating public policies.

There leave plenty of rooms for further research. For instance, similar approaches could be implemented to impute the true income distribution for other countries, in particular those emerging economics that suffer severe corruption problems. Also, if we are permitted more available detailed food consumption data like in-home dining expenditures, further steps could be taken to estimate the real aggregated income distribution with an enhancing validity. Using imputed true income also helps other economics research projects like evaluating tax reform policy near the end of 20th century. These meaningful and exciting projects certainly deserve future exploration.
